title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Groovy_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Groovy (programming language)">Groovy</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julia_(programming_language)" title="Julia (programming language)">Julia</a>, <a href="/wiki/LPC_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="LPC (programming language)">LPC</a>, <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">PHP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raku_(programming_language)" title="Raku (programming language)">Raku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)" title="Ruby (programming language)">Ruby</a>, <a href="/wiki/PowerShell" title="PowerShell">PowerShell</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below hlist" style="border-top: 1px solid #aaa; padding-top: 3px;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/16px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/24px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/32px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a> <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Perl_Programming" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Perl Programming">Perl Programming</a> at Wikibooks</li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Perl</b> is a family of two <a href="/wiki/High-level_programming_language" title="High-level programming language">high-level</a>, <a href="/wiki/General-purpose_programming_language" title="General-purpose programming language">general-purpose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interpreter_(computing)" title="Interpreter (computing)">interpreted</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language" title="Dynamic programming language">dynamic programming languages</a>. "Perl" refers to Perl 5, but from 2000 to 2019 it also referred to its redesigned "sister language", Perl 6, before the latter\'s name was officially changed to <a href="/wiki/Raku_(programming_language)" title="Raku (programming language)">Raku</a> in October 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Perl 7, continuing from Perl 5, not Perl 6, is also due in 2021.\n</p><p>Though Perl is not officially an acronym,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> there are various <a href="/wiki/Backronym" title="Backronym">backronyms</a> in use, including "Practical <a href="/wiki/Data_extraction" title="Data extraction">Extraction</a> and Reporting Language".<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Perl was originally developed by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Wall" title="Larry Wall">Larry Wall</a> in 1987 as a general-purpose <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> <a href="/wiki/Scripting_language" title="Scripting language">scripting language</a> to make report processing easier.<sup id="cite_ref-sheppard00_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sheppard00-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Since then, it has undergone many changes and revisions. Raku, which began as a redesign of Perl 5 in 2000, eventually evolved into a separate language. Both languages continue to be developed independently by different development teams and liberally borrow ideas from each other.\n</p><p>The Perl languages borrow features from other programming languages including <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shell_script" title="Shell script">shell script</a> (<a href="/wiki/Unix_shell" title="Unix shell">sh</a>), <a href="/wiki/AWK_(programming_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="AWK (programming language)">AWK</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sed" title="Sed">sed</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-perltimeline_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perltimeline-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Wall also alludes to <a href="/wiki/BASIC" title="BASIC">BASIC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language)" title="Lisp (programming language)">Lisp</a> in the introduction to <i><a href="/wiki/Learning_Perl" title="Learning Perl">Learning Perl</a></i> (Schwartz &amp; Christiansen) and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> They provide text processing facilities without the arbitrary data-length limits of many contemporary <a href="/wiki/List_of_Unix_commands" title="List of Unix commands">Unix command line tools</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-programmingperl2_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-programmingperl2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> facilitating manipulation of <a href="/wiki/Text_file" title="Text file">text files</a>. Perl 5 gained widespread popularity in the late 1990s as a <a href="/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface" title="Common Gateway Interface">CGI scripting</a> language, in part due to its unsurpassed <a href="/wiki/Regular_expression" title="Regular expression">regular expression</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="String (computing)">string</a> <a href="/wiki/Parsing" title="Parsing">parsing</a> abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-roderick02_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roderick02-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to CGI, Perl 5 is used for <a href="/wiki/System_administrator" title="System administrator">system administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_network_programming" title="Computer network programming">network programming</a>, finance, <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a>, and other applications, such as for <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">GUIs</a>. It has been nicknamed "the Swiss Army chainsaw of scripting languages" because of its flexibility and power,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and also its ugliness.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In 1998, it was also referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Duct_tape" title="Duct tape">duct tape</a> that holds the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> together," in reference to both its ubiquitous use as a <a href="/wiki/Glue_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Glue language">glue language</a> and its perceived inelegance.<sup id="cite_ref-leonard98_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leonard98-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl is a highly <a href="/wiki/Expressive_power_(computer_science)" title="Expressive power (computer science)">expressive</a> programming language: source code for a given algorithm can be short and highly compressible.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toctext">Features</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Design"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Design</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Applications"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Applications</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Implementation"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Implementation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Availability"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Availability</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-17"><a href="#Windows"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Windows</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Database_interfaces"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Database interfaces</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Comparative_performance"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Comparative performance</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Optimizing"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Optimizing</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Perl_5"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Perl 5</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Raku_(Perl_6)"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Raku (Perl 6)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Perl_7"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Perl 7</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Perl_community"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Perl community</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#State_of_the_Onion"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">State of the Onion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Perl_pastimes"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Perl pastimes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Perl_on_IRC"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Perl on IRC</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#CPAN_Acme"><span class="tocnumber">8.4</span> <span class="toctext">CPAN Acme</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Example_code"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Example code</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Criticism"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_versions">Early versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Larry_Wall" title="Larry Wall">Larry Wall</a> began work on Perl in 1987, while working as a programmer at <a href="/wiki/Unisys" title="Unisys">Unisys</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-programmingperl2_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-programmingperl2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and released version 1.0 to the comp.sources.misc <a href="/wiki/Newsgroup" class="mw-redirect" title="Newsgroup">newsgroup</a> on December 18, 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The language expanded rapidly over the next few years.\n</p><p>Perl 2, released in 1988, featured a better regular expression engine. Perl 3, released in 1989, added support for <a href="/wiki/Binary_data" title="Binary data">binary data</a> streams.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Originally, the only documentation for Perl was a single lengthy <a href="/wiki/Man_page" title="Man page">man page</a>. In 1991, <i><a href="/wiki/Programming_Perl" title="Programming Perl">Programming Perl</a></i>, known to many Perl programmers as the "Camel Book" because of its cover, was published and became the <i>de facto</i> reference for the language. At the same time, the Perl version number was bumped to 4, not to mark a major change in the language but to identify the version that was well documented by the book.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Perl_5">Early Perl 5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Perl 5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Perl_5_version_history" title="Perl 5 version history">Perl 5 version history</a></div>\n<p>Perl 4 went through a series of <a href="/wiki/Maintenance_release" title="Maintenance release">maintenance releases</a>, culminating in Perl 4.036 in 1993, whereupon Wall abandoned Perl 4 to begin work on Perl 5. Initial design of Perl 5 continued into 1994. The <i>perl5-porters</i> <a href="/wiki/Mailing_list" title="Mailing list">mailing list</a> was established in May 1994 to coordinate work on porting Perl 5 to different platforms. It remains the primary forum for development, maintenance, and porting of Perl 5.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5.000 was released on October 17, 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> It was a nearly complete rewrite of the <a href="/wiki/Interpreter_(computing)" title="Interpreter (computing)">interpreter</a>, and it added many new features to the language, including <a href="/wiki/Object_(computer_science)" title="Object (computer science)">objects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reference_(computer_science)" title="Reference (computer science)">references</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_variable" title="Local variable">lexical (my) variables</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Modular_programming" title="Modular programming">modules</a>. Importantly, modules provided a mechanism for extending the language without modifying the interpreter. This allowed the core interpreter to stabilize, even as it enabled ordinary Perl programmers to add new language features. Perl 5 has been in active development since then.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5.001 was released on March 13, 1995. Perl 5.002 was released on February 29, 1996 with the new prototypes feature. This allowed module authors to make <a href="/wiki/Subroutine" title="Subroutine">subroutines</a> that behaved like Perl <a href="/wiki/Shell_builtin" title="Shell builtin">builtins</a>. Perl 5.003 was released June 25, 1996, as a security release.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>One of the most important events in Perl 5 history took place outside of the language proper and was a consequence of its module support. On October 26, 1995, the <a href="/wiki/CPAN" title="CPAN">Comprehensive Perl Archive Network</a> (CPAN) was established as a <a href="/wiki/Software_repository" title="Software repository">repository</a> for the Perl language and <a href="/wiki/Perl_module" title="Perl module">Perl modules</a>; as of May 2017, it carries over 185,178 modules in 35,190 distributions, written by more than 13,071 authors, and is mirrored worldwide at more than 245 locations.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5.004 was released on May 15, 1997, and included, among other things, the UNIVERSAL package, giving Perl a base object from which all <a href="/wiki/Class_(computer_programming)" title="Class (computer programming)">classes</a> were automatically derived and the ability to require versions of modules. Another significant development was the inclusion of the <a href="/wiki/CGI.pm" title="CGI.pm">CGI.pm</a> module,<sup id="cite_ref-5004delta_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5004delta-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> which contributed to Perl\'s popularity as a <a href="/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface" title="Common Gateway Interface">CGI scripting language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-patwardhan02_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patwardhan02-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5.004 also added support for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> and several other operating systems.<sup id="cite_ref-5004delta_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5004delta-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5.005 was released on July 22, 1998. This release included several enhancements to the <a href="/wiki/Regular_expression" title="Regular expression">regex</a> engine, new hooks into the backend through the <code>B::*</code> modules, the <code>qr//</code> regex quote operator, a large selection of other new core modules, and added support for several more operating systems, including <a href="/wiki/BeOS" title="BeOS">BeOS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5005delta_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5005delta-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="2000.E2.80.932020"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2000\xe2\x80\x932020">2000\xe2\x80\x932020</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2000\xe2\x80\x932020">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Major version<sup style="background-color: #D4F4B4;" title="Current stable version" data-sort-value="5.32"><span style="display: none;">Current stable version:</span> <b>5.32</b>\n</td>\n<td>2021-01-23\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="templateVersion p" style="background-color: #C1E6F5;" title="Future release" data-sort-value="5.34"><span style="display: none;">Future none;">Future release:</span> 7.0\n</td>\n<td>2022?\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="99"><small><div class="templateVersion lv floatright" style=""><span style="display: none;"><span style="padding-left: 1.5em; white-space: nowrap;"><b>Legend:</b></span><br /></span><span style=""><span style="border-left: 1.2em solid #FDB3AB; padding-left: 0.3em; white-space: nowrap;" title="Old maintained">Older version, still maintained</span><br /></span><span style=""><span style="border-left: 1.2em solid #D4F4B4; padding-left: 0.3em; white-space: nowrap;" title="Current stable version"><b>Current stable version</b></span><br /></span><span style=""><span style="border-left: 1.2em solid #FED1A0; padding-left: 0.3em; white-space: nowrap;" title="Latest preview version of a future release">Latest preview version</span><br /></span><span style=""><span style="border-left: 1.2em solid #C1E6F5; padding-left: 0.3em; white-space: nowrap;" title="Future release">Future release</span></span></div></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Perl 5.6 was released on March 22, 2000.  Major changes included <a href="/wiki/64-bit_computing" title="64-bit computing">64-bit</a> support, <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> string representation, support for files over 2 GiB, and the "our" keyword.<sup id="cite_ref-56delta_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56delta-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-561delta_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-561delta-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  When developing Perl 5.6, the decision was made to switch the <a href="/wiki/Software_versioning" title="Software versioning">versioning</a> scheme to one more similar to other open source projects; after 5.005_63, the next version became 5.5.640, with plans for development versions to have odd numbers and stable versions to have even numbers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 2000, Wall put forth a call for suggestions for a new version of Perl from the community. The process resulted in 361 RFC (request for comments) documents that were to be used in guiding development of Perl 6. In 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> work began on the "Apocalypses" for Perl 6, a series of documents meant to summarize the change requests and present the design of the next generation of Perl. They were presented as a digest of the RFCs, rather than a formal document. At this point, Perl 6 existed only as a description of a language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5.8 was first released on July 18, 2002, and had nearly yearly updates since then. Perl 5.8 improved Unicode support, added a new I/O implementation, added a new thread implementation, improved numeric accuracy, and added several new modules.<sup id="cite_ref-perl58delta_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perl58delta-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> As of 2013 this version still remains the most popular version of Perl and is used by Red Hat 5, Suse 10, Solaris 10, HP-UX 11.31 and AIX 5.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 2004, work began on the "Synopses"&#160;&#8211;&#32;documents that originally summarized the Apocalypses, but which became the specification for the Perl 6 language. In February 2005, <a href="/wiki/Audrey_Tang" title="Audrey Tang">Audrey Tang</a> began work on <a href="/wiki/Pugs_(programming)" title="Pugs (programming)">Pugs</a>, a Perl 6 interpreter written in <a href="/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language)" title="Haskell (programming language)">Haskell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> This was the first concerted effort towards making Perl 6 a reality. This effort stalled in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 18, 2007, the 20th anniversary of Perl 1.0, Perl 5.10.0 was released. Perl 5.10.0 included notable new features, which brought it closer to Perl 6. These included a <a href="/wiki/Switch_statement" title="Switch statement">switch statement</a> (called "given"/"when"), regular expressions updates, and the \'<i>smart match operator</i> (~~).<sup id="cite_ref-5100delta_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5100delta-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-perlsyn-smart_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perlsyn-smart-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\nAround this same time, development began in earnest on another implementation of Perl 6 known as <a href="/wiki/Rakudo" title="Rakudo">Rakudo Perl</a>, developed in tandem with the <a href="/wiki/Parrot_virtual_machine" title="Parrot virtual machine">Parrot virtual machine</a>. As of November 2009, Rakudo Perl has had regular monthly releases and now is the most complete implementation of Perl 6.\n</p><p>A major change in the development process of Perl 5 occurred with Perl 5.11; the development community has switched to a monthly release cycle of development releases, with a yearly schedule of stable releases. By that plan, bugfix point releases will follow the stable releases every three months.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>On April 12, 2010, Perl 5.12.0 was released. Notable core enhancements include new <code>package NAME VERSION</code> syntax, the Yada Yada operator (intended to mark placeholder code that is not yet implemented), implicit strictures, full <a href="/wiki/Y2038" class="mw-redirect" title="Y2038">Y2038</a> compliance, regex conversion overloading, <a href="/wiki/DTrace" title="DTrace">DTrace</a> support, and <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> 5.2.<sup id="cite_ref-5120delta_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5120delta-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> On January 21, 2011, Perl 5.12.3 was released; it contains updated modules and some documentation changes.<sup id="cite_ref-5123delta_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5123delta-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Version 5.12.4 was released on June 20, 2011. The latest version of that branch, 5.12.5, was released on November 10, 2012.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>On May 14, 2011, Perl 5.14 was released. <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a> support is built-in as of 5.14.0.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The latest version of that branch, 5.14.4, was released on March 10, 2013.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>On May 20, 2012, Perl 5.16 was released. Notable new features include the ability to specify a given version of Perl that one wishes to emulate, allowing users to upgrade their version of Perl, but still run old scripts that would normally be incompatible.<sup id="cite_ref-5160delta_version_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5160delta_version-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Perl 5.16 also updates the core to support <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> 6.1.<sup id="cite_ref-5160delta_version_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5160delta_version-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On May 18, 2013, Perl 5.18 was released. Notable new features include the new dtrace hooks, lexical subs, more CORE:: subs, overhaul of the hash for security reasons, support for Unicode 6.2.<sup id="cite_ref-5180delta_version_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5180delta_version-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On May 27, 2014, Perl 5.20 was released. Notable new features include subroutine signatures, hash slices/new slice syntax, postfix dereferencing (experimental), Unicode 6.3, rand() using consistent random number generator.<sup id="cite_ref-5200delta_version_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5200delta_version-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some observers credit the release of Perl 5.10 with the start of the Modern Perl movement.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In particular, this phrase describes a style of development that embraces the use of the CPAN, takes advantage of recent developments in the language, and is rigorous about creating high quality code.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> While the book "Modern Perl"<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> may be the most visible standard-bearer of this idea, other groups such as the Enlightened Perl Organization<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> have taken up the cause.\n</p><p>In late 2012 and 2013, several projects for alternative implementations for Perl 5 started: Perl5 in <a href="/wiki/Raku_(programming_language)" title="Raku (programming language)">Perl6</a> by the Rakudo Perl team,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <i>moe</i> by Stevan Little and friends,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> <i>p2</i><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> by the Perl11 team under Reini Urban, <i>gperl</i> by goccy,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and <i>rperl</i> a kickstarter project led by Will Braswell and affiliated with the Perll11 project.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2020_onwards">2020 onwards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 2020 onwards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In June 2020, <a href="#Perl_7">Perl 7</a> was announced as the successor to Perl 5.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Perl 7 will initially be based on Perl 5.32 with a release expected in first half of 2021, and release candidates sooner.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> When Perl 7 is released, Perl 5 will go into long term maintenance. Supported Perl 5 versions however will continue to get important security and bug fixes.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PONIE">PONIE</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: PONIE">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>PONIE</b> is an acronym for Perl On New Internal Engine. The PONIE Project existed from 2003 until 2006 and was to be a bridge between Perl 5 and Perl 6. It was an effort to rewrite the Perl 5 interpreter to run on <a href="/wiki/Parrot_virtual_machine" title="Parrot virtual machine">Parrot</a>, the Perl 6 virtual machine. The goal was to ensure the future of the millions of lines of Perl 5 code at thousands of companies around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The PONIE project ended in 2006 and is no longer being actively developed.  Some of the improvements made to the Perl 5 interpreter as part of PONIE were folded into that project.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Perl was originally named "Pearl". Wall wanted to give the language a short name with positive connotations; he claims that he considered every three- and four-letter word in the dictionary. He also considered naming it after his wife Gloria. Wall discovered the existing <a href="/wiki/PEARL_(programming_language)" title="PEARL (programming language)">PEARL</a> programming language before Perl\'s official release and changed the spelling of the name.<sup id="cite_ref-richardson1999_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richardson1999-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When referring to the language, the name is normally capitalized (<i>Perl</i>) as a proper noun. When referring to the interpreter program itself, the name is often uncapitalized (<i>perl</i>) because most Unix-like file systems are case-sensitive. Before the release of the first edition of <i>Programming Perl</i>, it was common to refer to the language as <i>perl</i>; <a href="/wiki/Randal_L._Schwartz" title="Randal L. Schwartz">Randal L. Schwartz</a>, however, capitalized the language\'s name in the book to make it stand out better when typeset. This case distinction was subsequently documented as canonical.<sup id="cite_ref-capitalization_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capitalization-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The name is occasionally expanded as <i>Practical Extraction and Report Language</i>, but this is a <a href="/wiki/Backronym" title="Backronym">backronym</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Other expansions have been suggested as equally canonical, including Wall\'s own <i>Pathologically Eclectic Rubbish Lister</i> which is in the <a href="/wiki/Man_page" title="Man page">manual page</a> for perl.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, Wall claims that the name was intended to inspire many different expansions.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Camel_symbol">Camel symbol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Camel symbol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:146px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Perl-camel-small.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/00/Perl-camel-small.png" decoding="async" width="144" height="149" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="144" data-file-height="149" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Perl-camel-small.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Camel symbol used by O\'Reilly Media</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Programming Perl</i>, published by <a href="/wiki/O%27Reilly_Media" title="O&#39;Reilly Media">O\'Reilly Media</a>, features a picture of a <a href="/wiki/Dromedary_camel" class="mw-redirect" title="Dromedary camel">dromedary camel</a> on the cover and is commonly called the "Camel Book".<sup id="cite_ref-schwartz01_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schwartz01-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> This image of a camel has become an unofficial symbol of Perl as well as a general <a href="/wiki/Hacker_(programmer_subculture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hacker (programmer subculture)">hacker</a> emblem, appearing on T-shirts and other clothing items.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>O\'Reilly owns the image as a <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> but licenses it for <a href="/wiki/Non-commercial" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-commercial">non-commercial</a> use, requiring only an acknowledgement and a link to www.perl.com. Licensing for commercial use is decided on a case-by-case basis.<sup id="cite_ref-camel_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-camel-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> O\'Reilly also provides "Programming Republic of Perl" logos for non-commercial sites and "Powered by Perl" buttons for any site that uses Perl.<sup id="cite_ref-camel_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-camel-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Onion_symbol">Onion symbol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Onion symbol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:66px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Onion_64x64.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a4/Onion_64x64.png" decoding="async" width="64" height="64" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Onion_64x64.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The onion logo used by The Perl Foundation</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/The_Perl_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="The Perl Foundation">The Perl Foundation</a> owns an alternative symbol, an onion, which it licenses to its subsidiaries, <a href="/wiki/Perl_Mongers" title="Perl Mongers">Perl Mongers</a>, <a href="/wiki/PerlMonks" title="PerlMonks">PerlMonks</a>, Perl.org, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-onion_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onion-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The symbol is a <a href="/wiki/Visual_pun" title="Visual pun">visual pun</a> on <a href="/wiki/Pearl_onion" title="Pearl onion">pearl onion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gillmore98_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillmore98-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Raptor_symbol">Raptor symbol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Raptor symbol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:187px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Perl5Raptor.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Perl5Raptor.png" decoding="async" width="185" height="64" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="185" data-file-height="64" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Perl5Raptor.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alternative Perl 5 Logo</div></div></div>\n<p>Sebastian Riedel, the creator of <a href="/wiki/Mojolicious" title="Mojolicious">Mojolicious</a>, has created a logo depicting a <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">raptor dinosaur</a>, which is available under a CC-SA License, Version 4.0.<sup id="cite_ref-riedel11_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riedel11-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The logo is being remixed and used in different places and is symbolising Perl 5. The analogue of the raptor comes from a series of talks given by Matt S Trout beginning in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-trout10_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trout10-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The talks were aimed at being more Perl 5 community-centric, in a period where Perl 6 was a hot topic.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Perl_language_structure" title="Perl language structure">Perl language structure</a></div>\n<p>According to Wall, Perl has two slogans. The first is "<a href="/wiki/There%27s_more_than_one_way_to_do_it" title="There&#39;s more than one way to do it">There\'s more than one way to do it</a>," commonly known as TMTOWTDI. The second slogan is "Easy things should be easy and hard things should be possible".<sup id="cite_ref-programmingperl2_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-programmingperl2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The overall structure of Perl derives broadly from C. Perl is <a href="/wiki/Procedural_programming" title="Procedural programming">procedural</a> in nature, with <a href="/wiki/Variable_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Variable (programming)">variables</a>, <a href="/wiki/Expression_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Expression (programming)">expressions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assignment_statement" class="mw-redirect" title="Assignment statement">assignment statements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">brace</a>-delimited <a href="/wiki/Block_(programming)" title="Block (programming)">blocks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Control_structure" class="mw-redirect" title="Control structure">control structures</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subroutine" title="Subroutine">subroutines</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl also takes features from shell programming. All variables are marked with leading <a href="/wiki/Sigil_(computer_programming)" title="Sigil (computer programming)">sigils</a>, which allow variables to be <a href="/wiki/Variable_interpolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Variable interpolation">interpolated</a> directly into <a href="/wiki/String_(computer_science)" title="String (computer science)">strings</a>. However, unlike the shell, Perl uses sigils on all accesses to variables, and unlike most other programming languages that use sigils, the sigil doesn\'t denote the type of the variable but the type of the expression. So for example, while an array is denoted by the sigil "@" (for example <code>@arrayname</code>), an individual member of the array is denoted by the scalar sigil "$" (for example <code>$arrayname[3]</code>). Perl also has many built-in functions that provide tools often used in shell programming (although many of these tools are implemented by programs external to the shell) such as <a href="/wiki/Ascending_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascending order">sorting</a>, and calling <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> facilities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl takes <a href="/wiki/List_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="List (computing)">lists</a> from <a href="/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language)" title="Lisp (programming language)">Lisp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Associative_array" title="Associative array">hashes</a> ("associative arrays") from <a href="/wiki/AWK" title="AWK">AWK</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Regular_expression" title="Regular expression">regular expressions</a> from <a href="/wiki/Sed" title="Sed">sed</a>. These simplify and facilitate many parsing, text-handling, and data-management tasks. Also shared with Lisp are the implicit <a href="/wiki/Return_statement" title="Return statement">return</a> of the last value in a block, and the fact that all statements have a value, and thus are also expressions and can be used in larger expressions themselves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl 5 added features that support complex <a href="/wiki/Data_structure" title="Data structure">data structures</a>, <a href="/wiki/First-class_function" title="First-class function">first-class functions</a> (that is, <a href="/wiki/Closure_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Closure (computer science)">closures</a> as values), and an object-oriented programming model. These include <a href="/wiki/Reference_(computer_science)" title="Reference (computer science)">references</a>, packages, class-based <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_dispatch" title="Dynamic dispatch">method dispatch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scope_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Scope (programming)">lexically scoped variables</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Compiler_directive" class="mw-redirect" title="Compiler directive">compiler directives</a> (for example, the <code>strict</code> <a href="/wiki/Directive_(programming)" title="Directive (programming)">pragma</a>). A major additional feature introduced with Perl 5 was the ability to package code as reusable modules. Wall later stated that "The whole intent of Perl 5\'s module system was to encourage the growth of Perl culture rather than the Perl core."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All versions of Perl do automatic <a href="/wiki/Type_system" title="Type system">data-typing</a> and automatic <a href="/wiki/Memory_management" title="Memory management">memory management</a>. The interpreter knows the type and <a href="/wiki/Computer_data_storage" title="Computer data storage">storage</a> requirements of every data object in the program; it allocates and frees storage for them as necessary using <a href="/wiki/Reference_counting" title="Reference counting">reference counting</a> (so it cannot deallocate <a href="/wiki/Circular_dependency" title="Circular dependency">circular data structures</a> without manual intervention). Legal <a href="/wiki/Type_conversion" title="Type conversion">type conversions</a> \xe2\x80\x94 for example, conversions from number to string \xe2\x80\x94 are done automatically at <a href="/wiki/Run_time_(program_lifecycle_phase)" class="mw-redirect" title="Run time (program lifecycle phase)">run time</a>; illegal type conversions are fatal errors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The design of Perl can be understood as a response to three broad trends in the computer industry: falling hardware costs, rising labor costs, and improvements in <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> technology. Many earlier computer languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Fortran" title="Fortran">Fortran</a> and C, aimed to make efficient use of expensive computer hardware. In contrast, Perl was designed so that computer programmers could write programs more quickly and easily.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl has many features that ease the task of the programmer at the expense of greater <a href="/wiki/CPU" class="mw-redirect" title="CPU">CPU</a> and memory requirements. These include automatic memory management; <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_typing" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic typing">dynamic typing</a>; strings, lists, and hashes; regular expressions; <a href="/wiki/Type_introspection" title="Type introspection">introspection</a>; and an <code>eval()</code> function. Perl follows the theory of "no built-in limits,"<sup id="cite_ref-schwartz01_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schwartz01-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> an idea similar to the <a href="/wiki/Zero_One_Infinity" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero One Infinity">Zero One Infinity</a> rule.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Wall was trained as a linguist, and the design of Perl is very much informed by <a href="/wiki/Linguistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguistic">linguistic</a> principles. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Huffman_coding" title="Huffman coding">Huffman coding</a> (common constructions should be short), good end-weighting (the important information should come first), and a large collection of <a href="/wiki/Language_primitive" title="Language primitive">language primitives</a>. Perl favors language constructs that are concise and natural for humans to write, even where they complicate the Perl interpreter.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl\'s <a href="/wiki/Syntax_(programming_languages)" title="Syntax (programming languages)">syntax</a> reflects the idea that "things that are different should look different."<sup id="cite_ref-wall97_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wall97-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> For example, scalars, arrays, and hashes have different leading sigils. Array indices and hash keys use different kinds of braces. Strings and regular expressions have different standard delimiters. This approach can be contrasted with a language such as <a href="/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language)" title="Lisp (programming language)">Lisp</a>, where the same basic syntax, composed of simple and universal <a href="/wiki/S-expression" title="S-expression">symbolic expressions</a>, is used for all purposes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl does not enforce any particular <a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">programming paradigm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Procedural_programming" title="Procedural programming">procedural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented</a>, <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional</a>, or others) or even require the programmer to choose among them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>There is a broad practical bent to both the Perl language and the community and culture that surround it. The preface to <i>Programming Perl</i> begins: "Perl is a language for getting your job done."<sup id="cite_ref-programmingperl2_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-programmingperl2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> One consequence of this is that Perl is not a tidy language. It includes many features, tolerates exceptions to its rules, and employs <a href="/wiki/Heuristics" class="mw-redirect" title="Heuristics">heuristics</a> to resolve syntactical ambiguities. Because of the forgiving nature of the compiler, bugs can sometimes be hard to find. Perl\'s function documentation remarks on the variant behavior of built-in functions in list and scalar contexts by saying, "In general, they do what you want, unless you want consistency."<sup id="cite_ref-perlfunc_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perlfunc-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>No written <a href="/wiki/Formal_specification" title="Formal specification">specification</a> or standard for the Perl language exists for Perl versions through Perl 5, and there are no plans to create one for the current version of Perl. There has been only one implementation of the interpreter, and the language has evolved along with it. That interpreter, together with its functional tests, stands as a <i>de facto</i> specification of the language. Perl 6, however, started with a specification,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and several projects<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> aim to implement some or all of the specification.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Perl has many and varied applications, compounded by the availability of many standard and third-party modules.\n</p><p>Perl has chiefly been used to write <a href="/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface" title="Common Gateway Interface">CGI</a> scripts: large projects written in Perl include <a href="/wiki/CPanel" title="CPanel">cPanel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slash_(weblog_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="Slash (weblog system)">Slash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bugzilla" title="Bugzilla">Bugzilla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Request_Tracker" title="Request Tracker">RT</a>, <a href="/wiki/TWiki" title="TWiki">TWiki</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Movable_Type" title="Movable Type">Movable Type</a>; high-traffic websites that use Perl extensively include <a href="/wiki/Priceline.com" title="Priceline.com">Priceline.com</a>, <a href="/wiki/Craigslist" title="Craigslist">Craigslist</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Internet_Movie_Database" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet Movie Database">IMDb</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/LiveJournal" title="LiveJournal">LiveJournal</a>, <a href="/wiki/DuckDuckGo" title="DuckDuckGo">DuckDuckGo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slashdot" title="Slashdot">Slashdot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ticketmaster" title="Ticketmaster">Ticketmaster</a>. \nIt is also an optional component of the popular <a href="/wiki/LAMP_(software_bundle)" title="LAMP (software bundle)">LAMP</a> technology stack for <a href="/wiki/Web_development" title="Web development">Web development</a>, in lieu of <a href="/wiki/PHP" title="PHP">PHP</a> or <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>. Perl is used extensively as a <a href="/wiki/System_programming_language" title="System programming language">system programming language</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">Debian</a> GNU/Linux distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl is often used as a <a href="/wiki/Glue_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Glue language">glue language</a>, tying together systems and interfaces that were not specifically designed to interoperate, and for "<a href="/wiki/Data_munging" class="mw-redirect" title="Data munging">data munging</a>,"<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> that is, converting or processing large amounts of data for tasks such as creating reports. In fact, these strengths are intimately linked. The combination makes Perl a popular all-purpose language for <a href="/wiki/System_administrator" title="System administrator">system administrators</a>, particularly because short programs, often called "<a href="/wiki/One-liner_program" title="One-liner program">one-liner programs</a>," can be entered and run on a single <a href="/wiki/Command-line_interface" title="Command-line interface">command line</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl code can be made portable across <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a> and Unix; such code is often used by suppliers of software (both <a href="/wiki/Commercial_off-the-shelf" title="Commercial off-the-shelf">COTS</a> and bespoke) to simplify packaging and maintenance of software build- and deployment-scripts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">Graphical user interfaces</a> (GUIs) may be developed using Perl. For example, <a href="/wiki/Tk_(framework)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tk (framework)">Perl/Tk</a> and <a href="/wiki/WxPerl" class="mw-redirect" title="WxPerl">wxPerl</a> are commonly used to enable user interaction with Perl scripts. Such interaction may be synchronous or asynchronous, using <a href="/wiki/Callback_(computer_programming)" title="Callback (computer programming)">callbacks</a> to update the GUI.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Implementation">Implementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Implementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Perl is implemented as a core interpreter, written in C, together with a large collection of modules, written in Perl and C. As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the interpreter is 150,000 lines of C code and compiles to a 1&#160;MB executable on typical machine architectures. Alternatively, the interpreter can be compiled to a link library and embedded in other programs. There are nearly 500 modules in the distribution, comprising 200,000 lines of Perl and an additional 350,000 lines of C code (much of the C code in the modules consists of <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encoding</a> tables).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The interpreter has an object-oriented architecture. All of the elements of the Perl language\xe2\x80\x94scalars, arrays, hashes, coderefs, <a href="/wiki/File_handle" class="mw-redirect" title="File handle">file handles</a>\xe2\x80\x94are represented in the interpreter by <a href="/wiki/Struct_(C_programming_language)" title="Struct (C programming language)">C structs</a>. Operations on these structs are defined by a large collection of <a href="/wiki/Macro_(computer_science)" title="Macro (computer science)">macros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typedef" title="Typedef">typedefs</a>, and functions; these constitute the Perl C <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" class="mw-redirect" title="Application programming interface">API</a>. The Perl API can be bewildering to the uninitiated, but its entry points follow a consistent <a href="/wiki/Naming_scheme" class="mw-redirect" title="Naming scheme">naming scheme</a>, which provides guidance to those who use it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The life of a Perl interpreter divides broadly into a compile phase and a run phase.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>  In Perl, the <b>phases</b> are the major stages in the interpreter\'s life-cycle. Each interpreter goes through each phase only once, and the phases follow in a fixed sequence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Most of what happens in Perl\'s compile phase is compilation, and most of what happens in Perl\'s run phase is execution, but there are significant exceptions. Perl makes important use of its capability to execute Perl code during the compile phase. Perl will also delay compilation into the run phase. The terms that indicate the kind of processing that is actually occurring at any moment are <b>compile time</b> and <b>run time</b>.  Perl is in compile time at most points during the compile phase, but compile time may also be entered during the run phase. The compile time for code in a string argument passed to the <code><a href="/wiki/Eval" title="Eval">eval</a></code> built-in occurs during the run phase. Perl is often in run time during the compile phase and spends most of the run phase in run time.  Code in <code>BEGIN</code> blocks executes at run time but in the compile phase.\n</p><p>At compile time, the interpreter parses Perl code into a <a href="/wiki/Abstract_syntax_tree" title="Abstract syntax tree">syntax tree</a>. At run time, it executes the program by <a href="/wiki/Tree_traversal" title="Tree traversal">walking the tree</a>. Text is parsed only once, and the syntax tree is subject to optimization before it is executed, so that execution is relatively efficient. Compile-time optimizations on the syntax tree include <a href="/wiki/Constant_folding" title="Constant folding">constant folding</a> and context propagation, but <a href="/wiki/Peephole_optimization" title="Peephole optimization">peephole optimization</a> is also performed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Perl has a <a href="/wiki/Turing-complete" class="mw-redirect" title="Turing-complete">Turing-complete</a> <a href="/wiki/Formal_grammar" title="Formal grammar">grammar</a> because parsing can be affected by run-time code executed during the compile phase.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, Perl cannot be parsed by a straight <a href="/wiki/Lex_programming_tool" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex programming tool">Lex</a>/<a href="/wiki/Yacc" title="Yacc">Yacc</a> <a href="/wiki/Lexical_analysis" title="Lexical analysis">lexer</a>/<a href="/wiki/Parser" class="mw-redirect" title="Parser">parser</a> combination. Instead, the interpreter implements its own lexer, which coordinates with a modified <a href="/wiki/GNU_bison" class="mw-redirect" title="GNU bison">GNU bison</a> parser to resolve ambiguities in the language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>It is often said that "Only perl can parse Perl,"<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> meaning that only the Perl interpreter (<i><code>perl</code></i>) can parse the Perl language (<i>Perl</i>), but even this is not, in general, true. Because the Perl interpreter can simulate a Turing machine during its compile phase, it would need to decide the <a href="/wiki/Halting_problem" title="Halting problem">halting problem</a> in order to complete parsing in every case. It is a longstanding result that the halting problem is undecidable, and therefore not even perl can always parse Perl. Perl makes the unusual choice of giving the user access to its full programming power in its own compile phase. The cost in terms of theoretical purity is high, but practical inconvenience seems to be rare.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Other programs that undertake to parse Perl, such as <a href="/wiki/Static_program_analysis" title="Static program analysis">source-code analyzers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indent_style" class="mw-redirect" title="Indent style">auto-indenters</a>, have to contend not only with ambiguous <a href="/wiki/Language_construct" title="Language construct">syntactic constructs</a> but also with the <a href="/wiki/Recursive_language" title="Recursive language">undecidability</a> of Perl parsing in the general case. <a href="/wiki/Adam_Kennedy_(programmer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adam Kennedy (programmer)">Adam Kennedy</a>\'s PPI project focused on parsing Perl code as a document (retaining its integrity as a document), instead of parsing Perl as executable code (that not even Perl itself can always do). It was Kennedy who first conjectured that "parsing Perl suffers from the \'halting problem\',"<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> which was later proved.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perl is distributed with over 250,000 <a href="/wiki/Functional_testing" title="Functional testing">functional tests</a> for core Perl language and over 250,000 functional tests for core modules. These run as part of the normal build process and extensively exercise the interpreter and its core modules. Perl developers rely on the functional tests to ensure that changes to the interpreter do not introduce <a href="/wiki/Software_bug" title="Software bug">software bugs</a>; additionally, Perl users who see that the interpreter passes its functional tests on their system can have a high degree of confidence that it is working properly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Availability">Availability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Availability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Perl is <a href="/wiki/Dual_licensed" class="mw-redirect" title="Dual licensed">dual licensed</a> under both the <a href="/wiki/Artistic_License" title="Artistic License">Artistic License</a> 1.0<sup id="cite_ref-artistic-1.0_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artistic-1.0-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-artistic-1.0-git_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artistic-1.0-git-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">GNU General Public License</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-licensing_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-licensing-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Distributions are available for most <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>. It is particularly prevalent on <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> systems, but it has been ported to most modern (and many obsolete) platforms. With only six<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> reported exceptions, Perl can be compiled from <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">source code</a> on all <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">POSIX</a>-compliant, or otherwise-Unix-compatible, platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-cpanports_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cpanports-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of unusual changes required for the <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">classic Mac OS</a> environment, a special port called MacPerl was shipped independently.<sup id="cite_ref-macperl_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macperl-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/CPAN" title="CPAN">Comprehensive Perl Archive Network</a> carries a complete list of supported platforms with links to the distributions available on each.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> CPAN is also the source for publicly available Perl modules that are not part of the core Perl distribution.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Windows">Windows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Windows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Users of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> typically install one of the native binary distributions of Perl for Win32, most commonly <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Perl" title="Strawberry Perl">Strawberry Perl</a> or <a href="/wiki/ActivePerl" title="ActivePerl">ActivePerl</a>. Compiling Perl from source code under Windows is possible, but most installations lack the requisite C compiler and build tools. This also makes it difficult to install modules from the CPAN, particularly those that are partially written in C.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>ActivePerl is a closed-source distribution from <a href="/wiki/ActiveState" title="ActiveState">ActiveState</a> that has regular releases that track the core Perl releases.<sup id="cite_ref-activestate_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-activestate-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The distribution previously included the <a href="/wiki/Perl_package_manager" title="Perl package manager">Perl package manager</a> (PPM),<sup id="cite_ref-activestateppm_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-activestateppm-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> a popular tool for installing, removing, upgrading, and managing the use of common Perl modules; however, this tool was discontinued as of ActivePerl 5.28.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Included also is <a href="/wiki/PerlScript" class="mw-redirect" title="PerlScript">PerlScript</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Windows_Script_Host" title="Windows Script Host">Windows Script Host</a> (WSH) engine implementing the Perl language.  <a href="/wiki/Visual_Perl" class="mw-redirect" title="Visual Perl">Visual Perl</a> is an ActiveState tool that adds Perl to the Visual Studio .NET development suite.  A <a href="/wiki/VBScript" title="VBScript">VBScript</a>-to-Perl converter, as well as a Perl compiler for Windows, and converters of <a href="/wiki/Awk" class="mw-redirect" title="Awk">awk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sed" title="Sed">sed</a> to Perl have also been produced by this company and included on the <i>ActiveState CD for Windows</i>, which includes all of their distributions plus the <a href="/wiki/Komodo_IDE" title="Komodo IDE">Komodo IDE</a> and all but the first on the Unix/Linux/Posix variant thereof in 2002 and subsequently.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Strawberry Perl is an open-source distribution for Windows.  It has had regular, quarterly releases since January 2008, including new modules as feedback and requests come in.  Strawberry Perl aims to be able to install modules like standard Perl distributions on other platforms, including compiling XS modules.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cygwin" title="Cygwin">Cygwin</a> emulation layer is another way of running Perl under Windows. Cygwin provides a Unix-like environment on Windows, and both Perl and CPAN are available as standard pre-compiled packages in the Cygwin setup program. Since Cygwin also includes <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">gcc</a>, compiling Perl from source is also possible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>A perl executable is included in several Windows Resource kits in the directory with other scripting tools.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Implementations of Perl come with the <a href="/wiki/MKS_Toolkit" title="MKS Toolkit">MKS Toolkit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interix" title="Interix">Interix</a> (the base of earlier implementations of <a href="/wiki/Windows_Services_for_Unix" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows Services for Unix">Windows Services for Unix</a>), and <a href="/wiki/UWIN" title="UWIN">UWIN</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Database_interfaces">Database interfaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Database interfaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Perl\'s text-handling capabilities can be used for generating <a href="/wiki/SQL" title="SQL">SQL</a> queries; arrays, hashes, and automatic memory management make it easy to collect and process the returned data. For example, in Tim Bunce\'s <a href="/wiki/Perl_DBI" title="Perl DBI">Perl DBI</a> <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" class="mw-redirect" title="Application programming interface">application programming interface</a> (API), the arguments to the API can be the text of SQL queries; thus it is possible to program in multiple languages at the same time (e.g., for generating a <a href="/wiki/Web_page" title="Web page">Web page</a> using <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a>, <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, and SQL in a <a href="/wiki/Here_document" title="Here document">here document</a>).
