The use of Perl <a href="/wiki/Variable_interpolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Variable interpolation">variable interpolation</a> to programmatically customize each of the SQL queries, and the specification of Perl arrays or hashes as the structures to programmatically hold the resulting <a href="/wiki/Data_set" title="Data set">data sets</a> from each SQL query, allows a high-level mechanism for handling large amounts of data for post-processing by a Perl subprogram.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\nIn early versions of Perl, database interfaces were created by relinking the interpreter with a <a href="/wiki/Client-side" title="Client-side">client-side</a> database library. This was sufficiently difficult that it was done for only a few of the most-important and most widely used databases, and it restricted the resulting <code>perl</code> executable to using just one database interface at a time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In Perl 5, database interfaces are implemented by Perl DBI modules. The DBI (Database Interface) module presents a single, database-independent interface to Perl applications, while the DBD (Database Driver) modules handle the details of accessing some 50 different databases; there are DBD drivers for most <a href="/wiki/American_National_Standards_Institute" title="American National Standards Institute">ANSI</a> <a href="/wiki/SQL" title="SQL">SQL</a> databases.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>DBI provides caching for database handles and queries, which can greatly improve performance in long-lived execution environments such as <a href="/wiki/Mod_perl" title="Mod perl">mod perl</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> helping high-volume systems avert load spikes as in the <a href="/wiki/Slashdot_effect" title="Slashdot effect">Slashdot effect</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In modern Perl applications, especially those written using <a href="/wiki/Web_framework" title="Web framework">web frameworks</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Catalyst_(software)" title="Catalyst (software)">Catalyst</a>, the DBI module is often used indirectly via <a href="/wiki/Object-relational_mapper" class="mw-redirect" title="Object-relational mapper">object-relational mappers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/DBIx::Class" title="DBIx::Class">DBIx::Class</a>, Class::DBI<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> or Rose::DB::Object<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> that generate SQL queries and handle data transparently to the application author.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_performance">Comparative performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Comparative performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/The_Computer_Language_Benchmarks_Game" title="The Computer Language Benchmarks Game">The Computer Language Benchmarks Game</a> compares the performance of implementations of typical programming problems in several programming languages.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The submitted Perl implementations typically perform toward the high end of the memory-usage spectrum and give varied speed results. Perl\'s performance in the benchmarks game is typical for interpreted languages.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large Perl programs start more slowly than similar programs in compiled languages because perl has to compile the source every time it runs. In a talk at the <a href="/wiki/Yet_Another_Perl_Conference" title="Yet Another Perl Conference">YAPC::Europe 2005</a> conference and subsequent article "A Timely Start," Jean-Louis Leroy found that his Perl programs took much longer to run than expected because the perl interpreter spent significant time finding modules within his over-large include path.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Unlike Java, Python, and Ruby, Perl has only experimental support for pre-compiling.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, Perl programs pay this overhead penalty on every execution. The run phase of typical programs is long enough that <a href="/wiki/Amortized" class="mw-redirect" title="Amortized">amortized</a> startup time is not substantial, but benchmarks that measure very short execution times are likely to be skewed due to this overhead.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>A number of tools have been introduced to improve this situation. The first such tool was Apache\'s <a href="/wiki/Mod_perl" title="Mod perl">mod perl</a>, which sought to address one of the most-common reasons that small Perl programs were invoked rapidly: <a href="/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface" title="Common Gateway Interface">CGI</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">Web</a> development. <a href="/wiki/ActiveState" title="ActiveState">ActivePerl</a>, via Microsoft <a href="/wiki/ISAPI" class="mw-redirect" title="ISAPI">ISAPI</a>, provides similar performance improvements.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Once Perl code is compiled, there is additional overhead during the execution phase that typically isn\'t present for programs written in compiled languages such as C or C++. Examples of such overhead include <a href="/wiki/Bytecode" title="Bytecode">bytecode</a> interpretation, reference-counting memory management, and dynamic type-checking.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optimizing">Optimizing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Optimizing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Because Perl is an interpreted language, it can <a href="/wiki/Interpreted_language#Disadvantages" class="mw-redirect" title="Interpreted language">give problems</a> when efficiency is critical; in such situations, the most critical routines can be written in other languages (such as <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>), which can be connected to Perl via simple Inline modules or the more complex but flexible <a href="/wiki/XS_(Perl)" title="XS (Perl)">XS</a> mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Perl_5">Perl 5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Perl 5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Perl 5, the language usually referred to as "Perl", continues to be actively developed. Perl 5.12.0 was released in April 2010 with some new features influenced by the design of Perl 6,<sup id="cite_ref-5120delta_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5120delta-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-heise2010_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heise2010-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> followed by Perl 5.14.1 (released on June 17, 2011), Perl 5.16.1 (released on August 9, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>), and Perl 5.18.0 (released on May 18, 2013). Perl 5 development versions are released on a monthly basis, with major releases coming out once per year.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The relative proportion of Internet searches for "Perl programming", as compared with similar searches for other programming languages, steadily declined from about 10% in 2005 to about 2% in 2011, to about 0.7% in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Raku_.28Perl_6.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Raku_(Perl_6)">Raku (Perl 6)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Raku (Perl 6)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Raku_(programming_language)" title="Raku (programming language)">Raku (programming language)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Camelia.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Camelia.svg/200px-Camelia.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Camelia.svg/300px-Camelia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Camelia.svg/400px-Camelia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="693" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Camelia.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Camelia</i>, the logo for the Perl 6 project.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>At the 2000 <a href="/wiki/O%27Reilly_Open_Source_Convention" title="O&#39;Reilly Open Source Convention">Perl Conference</a>, Jon Orwant made a case for a major new language-initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This led to a decision to begin work on a redesign of the language, to be called Perl 6. Proposals for new language features were solicited from the Perl community at large, which submitted more than 300 <a href="/wiki/Request_for_Comments" title="Request for Comments">RFCs</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Wall spent the next few years digesting the RFCs and synthesizing them into a coherent framework for Perl 6. He presented his design for Perl 6 in a series of documents called "apocalypses"&#160;&#8211;&#32;numbered to correspond to chapters in <i>Programming Perl</i>. As of January&#160;2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the developing specification of Perl 6 was encapsulated in design documents called Synopses&#160;&#8211;&#32;numbered to correspond to Apocalypses.<sup id="cite_ref-syn6_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-syn6-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thesis work by <a href="/wiki/Bradley_M._Kuhn" title="Bradley M. Kuhn">Bradley M. Kuhn</a>, overseen by Wall, considered the possible use of the <a href="/wiki/Java_virtual_machine" title="Java virtual machine">Java virtual machine</a> as a runtime for Perl.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Kuhn\'s thesis showed this approach to be problematic. In 2001, it was decided that Perl 6 would run on a cross-language <a href="/wiki/Virtual_machine" title="Virtual machine">virtual machine</a> called <a href="/wiki/Parrot_virtual_machine" title="Parrot virtual machine">Parrot</a>. This will mean that other languages targeting the Parrot will gain native access to <a href="/wiki/CPAN" title="CPAN">CPAN</a>, allowing some level of cross-language development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, <a href="/wiki/Audrey_Tang" title="Audrey Tang">Audrey Tang</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Pugs_(programming)" title="Pugs (programming)">Pugs</a> project, an implementation of Perl 6 in <a href="/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language)" title="Haskell (programming language)">Haskell</a>. This acted as, and continues to act as, a test platform for the Perl 6 language (separate from the development of the actual implementation)&#160;&#8211;&#32;allowing the language designers to explore. The Pugs project spawned an active Perl/Haskell cross-language community centered around the <a href="/wiki/Freenode" title="Freenode">freenode</a> #perl6 IRC channel. Many <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional programming</a> influences were absorbed by the Perl 6 design team.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, Perl 6 development was centered primarily on two compilers:<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Rakudo" title="Rakudo">Rakudo</a>, an implementation running on the Parrot virtual machine and the Java virtual machine.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Niecza" class="mw-redirect" title="Niecza">Niecza</a>, which targets the <a href="/wiki/Common_Language_Runtime" title="Common Language Runtime">Common Language Runtime</a>.</li></ol>\n<p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/MoarVM" title="MoarVM">MoarVM</a> (\xe2\x80\x9cMetamodel On A Runtime\xe2\x80\x9d), a C language-based <a href="/wiki/Virtual_machine" title="Virtual machine">virtual machine</a> designed primarily for Rakudo was announced.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2019, Perl 6 was renamed to Raku.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> only the Rakudo implementation and MoarVM are under active development, and other virtual machines, such as the Java Virtual Machine and <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, are supported.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Perl_7">Perl 7</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Perl 7">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Perl 7 was announced on 24 June 2020 at "The Perl Conference in the Cloud" as the successor to Perl 5.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Based on Perl 5.32, Perl 7 is designed to be <a href="/wiki/Backward_compatibility" title="Backward compatibility">backwards compatible</a> with modern Perl 5 code; Perl 5 code, without <a href="/wiki/Boilerplate_code" title="Boilerplate code">boilerplate</a> (pragma) header needs adding <code>use compat::perl5;</code> to stay compatible, but modern code can drop some of the boilerplate.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Perl_community">Perl community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Perl community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Perl\'s culture and community has developed alongside the language itself. <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a> was the first public venue in which Perl was introduced, but over the course of its evolution, Perl\'s community was shaped by the growth of broadening Internet-based services including the introduction of the World Wide Web. The community that surrounds Perl was, in fact, the topic of Wall\'s first "State of the Onion" talk.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_of_the_Onion">State of the Onion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: State of the Onion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>State of the Onion is the name for Wall\'s yearly <a href="/wiki/Keynote" title="Keynote">keynote</a>-style summaries on the progress of Perl and its community.  They are characterized by his hallmark humor, employing references to Perl\'s culture, the wider hacker culture, Wall\'s linguistic background, sometimes his family life, and occasionally even his Christian background.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Each talk is first given at various Perl conferences and is eventually also published online.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perl_pastimes">Perl pastimes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Perl pastimes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dt>JAPHs</dt>\n<dd>In email, Usenet, and message board postings, "<a href="/wiki/Just_another_Perl_hacker" title="Just another Perl hacker">Just another Perl hacker</a>" (JAPH) programs are a common trend, originated by <a href="/wiki/Randal_L._Schwartz" title="Randal L. Schwartz">Randal L. Schwartz</a>, one of the earliest professional Perl trainers.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In the parlance of Perl culture, Perl programmers are known as Perl hackers, and from this derives the practice of writing short programs to print out the phrase "Just another Perl hacker". In the spirit of the original concept, these programs are moderately obfuscated and short enough to fit into the signature of an email or Usenet message. The "canonical" JAPH as developed by Schwartz includes the comma at the end, although this is often omitted.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Perl golf</dt>\n<dd>Perl "golf" is the pastime of reducing the number of characters (key "strokes") used in a Perl program to the bare minimum, much in the same way that <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a> players seek to take as few shots as possible in a round. The phrase\'s first use<sup id="cite_ref-perl-golf-coined_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perl-golf-coined-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> emphasized the difference between pedestrian code meant to teach a newcomer and terse hacks likely to amuse experienced Perl programmers, an example of the latter being <a href="/wiki/JAPH" class="mw-redirect" title="JAPH">JAPHs</a> that were already used in signatures in Usenet postings and elsewhere. Similar stunts had been an unnamed pastime in the language <a href="/wiki/APL_(programming_language)" title="APL (programming language)">APL</a> in previous decades. The use of Perl to write a program that performed <a href="/wiki/RSA_(algorithm)" class="mw-redirect" title="RSA (algorithm)">RSA</a> encryption prompted a widespread and practical interest in this pastime.<sup id="cite_ref-rsa_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsa-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In subsequent years, the term "<a href="/wiki/Code_golf" title="Code golf">code golf</a>" has been applied to the pastime in other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> A Perl Golf Apocalypse was held at Perl Conference 4.0 in Monterey, California in July 2000.</dd>\n<dt>Obfuscation</dt>\n<dd>As with C, <a href="/wiki/Obfuscated_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Obfuscated code">obfuscated code</a> competitions were a well known pastime in the late 1990s. The <a href="/wiki/Obfuscated_Perl_Contest" title="Obfuscated Perl Contest">Obfuscated Perl Contest</a> was a competition held by <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Perl_Journal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="The Perl Journal (page does not exist)">The Perl Journal</a> from 1996 to 2000 that made an arch virtue of Perl\'s syntactic flexibility. Awards were given for categories such as "most powerful"\xe2\x80\x94programs that made efficient use of space\xe2\x80\x94and "best four-line signature" for programs that fit into four lines of 76 characters in the style of a Usenet <a href="/wiki/Signature_block" title="Signature block">signature block</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gallo03_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gallo03-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Poetry</dt>\n<dd>Perl poetry is the practice of writing poems that can be compiled as legal Perl code, for example the piece known as <a href="/wiki/Black_Perl" title="Black Perl">Black Perl</a>. Perl poetry is made possible by the large number of English words that are used in the Perl language. New poems are regularly submitted to the community at <a href="/wiki/PerlMonks" title="PerlMonks">PerlMonks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perl_on_IRC">Perl on IRC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Perl on IRC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There are a number of <a href="/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat" title="Internet Relay Chat">IRC</a> channels that offer support for the language and some modules.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>IRC Network\n</th>\n<th>Channels\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.freenode.net\n</td>\n<td>#perl #perl6 #cbstream #perlcafe #poe\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.perl.org\n</td>\n<td>#moose #poe #catalyst #dbix-class #perl-help #distzilla #epo #corehackers #sdl #win32 #toolchain #padre #dancer\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.slashnet.org\n</td>\n<td>#perlmonks\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.oftc.net\n</td>\n<td>#perl\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.efnet.net\n</td>\n<td>#perlhelp\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.rizon.net\n</td>\n<td>#perl\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>irc.debian.org\n</td>\n<td>#debian-perl (packaging Perl modules for Debian)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CPAN_Acme">CPAN Acme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: CPAN Acme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There are also many examples of code written purely for entertainment on the <a href="/wiki/CPAN" title="CPAN">CPAN</a>. <code>Lingua::Romana::Perligata</code>, for example, allows writing programs in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Upon execution of such a program, the module translates its source code into regular Perl and runs it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The Perl community has set aside the "<a href="/wiki/Acme_Corporation" title="Acme Corporation">Acme</a>" namespace for modules that are fun in nature (but its scope has widened to include exploratory or experimental code or any other module that is not meant to ever be used in production). Some of the Acme modules are deliberately implemented in amusing ways. This includes <code>Acme::Bleach</code>, one of the first modules in the <code>Acme::</code> namespace,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> which allows the program\'s source code to be "whitened" (i.e., all characters replaced with whitespace) and yet still work.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_code">Example code</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Example code">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In older versions of Perl, one would write the <a href="/wiki/Hello_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Hello World">Hello World</a> program as:\n</p>\n<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-perl mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="k">print</span> <span class="s">&quot;Hello, World!\\n&quot;</span><span class="p">;</span>\n</pre></div>\n<p>Here is a more complex Perl program, that counts down seconds from a given starting value:\n</p>\n<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-perl mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="ch">#!/usr/bin/perl</span>\n<span class="k">use</span> <span class="nn">strict</span><span class="p">;</span>\n<span class="k">use</span> <span class="nn">warnings</span><span class="p">;</span>\n\n<span class="k">my</span> <span class="p">(</span> <span class="nv">$remaining</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nv">$total</span> <span class="p">);</span>\n\n<span class="nv">$remaining</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="nv">$total</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="nb">shift</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nv">@ARGV</span><span class="p">);</span>\n\n<span class="bp">STDOUT</span><span class="o">-&gt;</span><span class="n">autoflush</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">);</span>\n\n<span class="k">while</span> <span class="p">(</span> <span class="nv">$remaining</span> <span class="p">)</span> <span class="p">{</span>\n    <span class="nb">printf</span> <span class="p">(</span> <span class="s">&quot;Remaining %s/%s \\r&quot;</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nv">$remaining</span><span class="o">--</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nv">$total</span> <span class="p">);</span>\n    <span class="nb">sleep</span> <span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">;</span>\n<span class="p">}</span>\n\n<span class="k">print</span> <span class="s">&quot;\\n&quot;</span><span class="p">;</span>\n</pre></div>\n<p>The perl interpreter can also be used for one-off scripts on the command line. The following example (as invoked from an sh-compatible shell, such as Bash) translates the string "Bob" in all files ending with .txt in the current directory to "Robert":\n</p>\n<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-console mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="gp">$ </span>perl -i.bak -lp -e <span class="s1">&#39;s/Bob/Robert/g&#39;</span> *.txt\n</pre></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Perl has been referred to as "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/line_noise" class="extiw" title="wikt:line noise">line noise</a>" and a <a href="/wiki/Write-only_language" title="Write-only language">write-only language</a> by its critics. The earliest such mention was in the first edition of the book <i><a href="/wiki/Learning_Perl" title="Learning Perl">Learning Perl</a></i>, a Perl 4 tutorial book written by <a href="/wiki/Randal_L._Schwartz" title="Randal L. Schwartz">Randal L. Schwartz</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> in the first chapter of which he states: "Yes, sometimes Perl looks like line noise to the uninitiated, but to the seasoned Perl programmer, it looks like checksummed line noise with a mission in life."<sup id="cite_ref-LP_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> He also stated that the accusation that Perl is a write-only language could be avoided by coding with "proper care".<sup id="cite_ref-LP_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The Perl overview document <i>perlintro</i> states that the names of built-in "magic" scalar <a href="/wiki/Variable_(computer_science)" title="Variable (computer science)">variables</a> "look like punctuation or line noise".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> However, the English module provides  both long and short English alternatives. <i>perlstyle</i> document states that line noise in regular expressions could be mitigated using the <code>/x</code> modifier to add whitespace.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <i>Perl 6 FAQ</i>, Perl 6 was designed to mitigate "the usual suspects" that elicit the "line noise" claim from Perl 5 critics, including the removal of "the majority of the punctuation variables" and the sanitization of the regex syntax.<sup id="cite_ref-P6FAQ_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P6FAQ-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Perl 6 FAQ</i> also states that what is sometimes referred to as Perl\'s line noise is "the actual syntax of the language" just as <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">gerunds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">prepositions</a> are a part of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-P6FAQ_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P6FAQ-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In a December 2012 blog posting, despite claiming that "Rakudo Perl 6 has failed and will continue to fail unless it gets some adult supervision", <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_(programmer)" title="Chromatic (programmer)">chromatic</a> stated that the design of Perl 6 has a "well-defined grammar" as well as an "improved type system, a unified object system with an intelligent metamodel, metaoperators, and a clearer system of context that provides for such niceties as pervasive laziness".<sup id="cite_ref-chromatic-blog_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chromatic-blog-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> He also stated that "Perl 6 has a coherence and a consistency that Perl 5 lacks."<sup id="cite_ref-chromatic-blog_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chromatic-blog-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Perl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r936637989">.mw-parser-output .portal{border:solid #aaa 1px;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output
